Linguistic Meaning of Hadd: Prevention
Author provides linguistic foundation of hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the root meaning of hadd as 'prevention' and explain how it carries through various applications in Islamic law.

Core Idea
The entire concept of definition in usul al-fiqh is rooted in prevention.

Before exploring how scholars define a legal term, we must understand the linguistic foundation of the word 'hadd'. In Arabic, it means 'prevention', and this meaning illuminates why definitions must both include and exclude.

Guided Path
1
Identify the root meaning
The author says hadd means prevention (al-man'). Find this statement in the Arabic.
2
Trace the metaphor across applications
The author gives several examples: doorkeeper, iron, boundaries of property, legal punishment, mourning. Each illustrates prevention. Notice how each example relates to definition.
3
Connect linguistic to terminological
How might the linguistic sense of prevention prepare you for the terminological requirement that a definition must be 'comprehensive and preventive' (al-jāmi' al-māni')?

المبحث الثاني
الحد أو التعريف
المطلب الأول: مفهومهما عند الأصوليين
أ - الحد لغة واصطلاحًا
الحد، في اللغة؛ المنع ، ولذلك سمي البواب حدادًا، لأنه يمنع من ليس من أهل الدار من الدخول إليها .. وسموا الحديد بهذا الاسم، لأنه يمنع من وصول السلاح إلى المتحصن به .. وسميت حدود الدار والأرض، لأنها تمنع أن يدخل في البيع ما ليس من المبيع، وكذا يخرج منه ما ليس هو من المبيع . . وسميت العقوبة حدًا، لما فيها من المنع من مواقعة الفواحش . . ومنه : إحداد المرأة في عدتها، لأنها تمتنع به من الطيب والزينة
أما بالنسبة للأصوليين فقد تنوعت تعريفاتهم للحد : قال الباقلاني : حد كل أمر محدود هو تفسير وصف المسؤول عنه واسمه بقول جامع للمحدود، ومانع من أن يدخل فيه ما ليس منه، أو أن يخرج منه ما هو منه ويكون سامعه أقرب عند سماعه إلى معرفة معنى ما يسأل عنه وقد نقل الشيرازي تعريفا آخر للباقلاني، فقال: والعبارة الصحيحة في الحد ؛ عبارة القاضي أبي بكر الباقلاني، قال: هو العبارة عن المقصود بما يحصره ويحيط به إحاطة تمنع من أن يدخل فيه ما ليس هو منه وأن يخرج منه ما هو منه وقال الباجي : «الحد : اللفظ الجامع المانع». وقال أبو مظفر السمعاني : هو اللفظ الوجيز المحيط بالمعنى.
وقال أبو يعلى الفراء : معنى الحد : هو الجامع لجنس ما فرقه التفصيل المانع من دخول ما ليس من جملته .. والزيادة في الحد نقصان في المحدود لأن الحد متى جمع ذواتا كانت متفرقة حال التفصيل، فمتى ضم إليه قدر زائد على المذكور خرج بعض الذوات من جملة الكلام فيكون الحد للبعض بعد أن كان للجميع.
وقال أبو بكر الباقلاني : الزيادة فيه على ضربين منها ما هو نقصان منه، ومنها ما هو ليس بنقصان . فأما التي هي نقصان نحو قولك : حد الواجب أنه في فعله ثواب، وفي تركه عقاب، فهذا يوجب خروج كل ما ليس بصيام عن كونه واجبا، فعادت بالنقصان . وأما ما ليس بنقصان نحو قولك : حد الواجب ؛ أنه فرض في فعله ثواب وفي تركه عقاب، فكل فرض واجب . وأما النقصان من الحد، فإنه - أبدا - زيادة فيه نحو قولك : حد الواجب ما كان في فعله ثواب، ولا يقرن به في تركه عقاب فيدخل النفل في جملة الواجب، لأنه مما يثاب عليه
Second section: The boundary or definition. First subsection: Their concept among the usuliyyin. A - 'Hadd' linguistically and terminologically. 'Hadd' in language means prevention. Thus the doorkeeper is called 'haddād' because he prevents those who are not from the people of the house from entering it. They called iron by this name because it prevents the weapon from reaching the one fortified by it. The boundaries of a house and land were named thus because they prevent what is not part of the sale from entering the sale, and likewise they prevent what is part of the sale from exiting it. Punishment was called 'hadd' because of the prevention it contains from committing obscenities. From it is the mourning ('ihdād) of a woman during her waiting period because she refrains from perfume and adornment. As for the usuliyyin, their definitions of 'hadd' varied. Al-Baqillani said: The boundary of every bounded thing is the explanation of the description of what is asked about and its name, by a statement that is comprehensive of the bounded and prevents anything not from it from entering it, or prevents anything that is from it from exiting it, and its hearer becomes closer upon hearing it to knowing the meaning of what was asked about. Al-Shirazi transmitted another definition from al-Baqillani, saying: The correct expression for the boundary is the expression of al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani: it is the expression about the intended meaning by what encompasses it and surrounds it with a surrounding that prevents what is not from it from entering it and prevents what is from it from exiting it. Al-Baji said: 'The boundary is the comprehensive and preventive expression.' Abu Muzaffar al-Sam'ani said: It is the concise expression that encompasses the meaning. Abu Ya'la al-Farra' said: The meaning of the boundary is that which gathers the species that detail dispersed, preventing what is not from its entirety from entering ... And the increase in the boundary is a deficiency in the bounded, because when the boundary gathers entities that were separate in the state of detail, if an excess amount is added to the mentioned, some of the entities exit from the entirety of the statement, so the boundary becomes for some after it was for all. Abu Bakr al-Baqillani said: The increase in it is of two types: one is a deficiency in it, and one is not a deficiency. As for that which is a deficiency, like your saying: The boundary of the obligatory (wajib) is that there is reward in doing it and punishment in abandoning it. This necessitates the exit of everything that is not fasting from being obligatory, so it returns as deficiency. As for that which is not a deficiency, like your saying: The boundary of the obligatory is that it is an obligation (fard); there is reward in doing it and punishment in abandoning it, for every obligation is obligatory. As for the deficiency from the boundary, it is always an increase in it, like your saying: The boundary of the obligatory is what has reward in doing it, without coupling punishment in abandoning it, so the supererogatory (nafl) enters into the category of the obligatory, because it is something for which one is rewarded.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the concept of 'hadd' (definition/boundary) as understood by usuliyyin (scholars of Islamic jurisprudence). It begins with the linguistic meaning—'prevention'—illustrated through examples: the doorkeeper, iron, boundaries of property, legal punishments, and the mourning period. These examples show that the root meaning of 'hadd' carries the idea of demarcation and restriction. The passage then presents multiple terminological definitions from prominent usuliyyin. Al-Baqillani's first definition emphasizes a statement that comprehensively gathers the defined thing and prevents anything extraneous from entering or anything essential from exiting. His second definition, transmitted by al-Shirazi, stresses encompassing and surrounding the intended meaning. Al-Baji offers the concise phrase 'the comprehensive and preventive expression' (al-lafdh al-jāmi' al-māni'). Al-Sam'ani adds the requirement of conciseness (al-wajīz). Abu Ya'la al-Farra' focuses on gathering the dispersed species and preventing entry of what is not from the whole. This leads to a discussion on the effect of increase in wording. Abu Ya'la states that any increase in the definition causes a deficiency in coverage because when the definition originally covered all entities, adding more words excludes some. Al-Baqillani distinguishes two types of increase: one that is a deficiency (like defining 'wajib' as 'doing it brings reward, abandoning it brings punishment' excludes acts like fasting, which are also wajib but not specified) and one that is not a deficiency (like defining 'wajib' as 'fard', since every fard is wajib). He also notes that a deficiency in the definition (e.g., omitting punishment for abandonment) is actually an increase that includes non-obligatory acts (like supererogatory deeds). The overall aim is to establish that a proper definition must be both inclusive of all instances and exclusive of non-instances, and that wording must be carefully chosen to avoid unintentional narrowing or broadening.

الحد
المنع

C) Inheritance laws preventing property disputes
D) Punishment preventing crimes

Key Takeaway
The linguistic meaning of hadd—prevention—is the key to understanding why definitions must both include and exclude.

Terminological Definitions of Hadd by Usuliyyin
Author compiles definitions of hadd from key usuliyyin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare and contrast the definitions of hadd given by al-Baqillani, al-Baji, al-Sam'ani, and Abu Ya'la al-Farra', identifying the common requirement of comprehensiveness and prevention.

Core Idea
All usuliyyin agree that a definition must be both comprehensive and preventive.

Now that we know the linguistic meaning, we turn to how the great usuliyyin formulated the technical definition. Their definitions emphasize the dual function of gathering all relevant instances and excluding all irrelevant ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify each scholar's definition
Locate the definitions by al-Baqillani (two versions), al-Baji, al-Sam'ani, and Abu Ya'la. Write down the key phrases.
2
Extract the common elements
All definitions mention comprehensiveness (jāmi') and prevention (māni'). Notice how each scholar phrases it differently.
3
Note the differences
Al-Baqillani adds the effect on the listener; al-Baji is concise; al-Sam'ani adds conciseness (wajīz); Abu Ya'la talks about gathering species from detail. How do these differences highlight different concerns?

Hadd and Rasm
intermediate
7 min
Effect of Increase and Decrease in Definitions
Author discusses the paradox of increase and decrease in definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an increase in a definition that causes deficiency and one that does not, based on al-Baqillani's analysis.

Core Idea
Adding words can narrow a definition; omitting words can broaden it.

After establishing what a definition should be, the usuliyyin turned to the pitfalls. The most subtle is that a change in wording—even an addition—can actually make the definition narrower or broader than intended. This is what Abu Ya'la and al-Baqillani analyze.

Guided Path
1
Understand Abu Ya'la's general principle
He says 'the increase in the boundary is a deficiency in the bounded.' Why? Because if you add words, you might exclude some of the original items.
2
Examine al-Baqillani's two types of increase
He gives examples: defining wajib as 'reward in doing, punishment in leaving' is a deficiency (excludes fasting). But defining wajib as 'fard' is not a deficiency.
3
Analyze the deficiency from the boundary
Al-Baqillani says that a deficiency in the definition (e.g., omitting punishment for abandonment) is actually an increase (broadens to include supererogatory acts). Understand the inverse relationship.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني
الحد أو التعريف
المطلب الأول: مفهومهما عند الأصوليين
أ - الحد لغة واصطلاحًا
الحد، في اللغة؛ المنع ، ولذلك سمي البواب حدادًا، لأنه يمنع من ليس من أهل الدار من الدخول إليها .. وسموا الحديد بهذا الاسم، لأنه يمنع من وصول السلاح إلى المتحصن به .. وسميت حدود الدار والأرض، لأنها تمنع أن يدخل في البيع ما ليس من المبيع، وكذا يخرج منه ما ليس هو من المبيع . . وسميت العقوبة حدًا، لما فيها من المنع من مواقعة الفواحش . . ومنه : إحداد المرأة في عدتها، لأنها تمتنع به من الطيب والزينة
أما بالنسبة للأصوليين فقد تنوعت تعريفاتهم للحد : قال الباقلاني : حد كل أمر محدود هو تفسير وصف المسؤول عنه واسمه بقول جامع للمحدود، ومانع من أن يدخل فيه ما ليس منه، أو أن يخرج منه ما هو منه ويكون سامعه أقرب عند سماعه إلى معرفة معنى ما يسأل عنه وقد نقل الشيرازي تعريفا آخر للباقلاني، فقال: والعبارة الصحيحة في الحد ؛ عبارة القاضي أبي بكر الباقلاني، قال: هو العبارة عن المقصود بما يحصره ويحيط به إحاطة تمنع من أن يدخل فيه ما ليس هو منه وأن يخرج منه ما هو منه وقال الباجي : «الحد : اللفظ الجامع المانع». وقال أبو مظفر السمعاني : هو اللفظ الوجيز المحيط بالمعنى.
وقال أبو يعلى الفراء : معنى الحد : هو الجامع لجنس ما فرقه التفصيل المانع من دخول ما ليس من جملته .. والزيادة في الحد نقصان في المحدود لأن الحد متى جمع ذواتا كانت متفرقة حال التفصيل، فمتى ضم إليه قدر زائد على المذكور خرج بعض الذوات من جملة الكلام فيكون الحد للبعض بعد أن كان للجميع.
وقال أبو بكر الباقلاني : الزيادة فيه على ضربين منها ما هو نقصان منه، ومنها ما هو ليس بنقصان . فأما التي هي نقصان نحو قولك : حد الواجب أنه في فعله ثواب، وفي تركه عقاب، فهذا يوجب خروج كل ما ليس بصيام عن كونه واجبا، فعادت بالنقصان . وأما ما ليس بنقصان نحو قولك : حد الواجب ؛ أنه فرض في فعله ثواب وفي تركه عقاب، فكل فرض واجب . وأما النقصان من الحد، فإنه - أبدا - زيادة فيه نحو قولك : حد الواجب ما كان في فعله ثواب، ولا يقرن به في تركه عقاب فيدخل النفل في جملة الواجب، لأنه مما يثاب عليه
Second section: The boundary or definition. First subsection: Their concept among the usuliyyin. A - 'Hadd' linguistically and terminologically. 'Hadd' in language means prevention. Thus the doorkeeper is called 'haddād' because he prevents those who are not from the people of the house from entering it. They called iron by this name because it prevents the weapon from reaching the one fortified by it. The boundaries of a house and land were named thus because they prevent what is not part of the sale from entering the sale, and likewise they prevent what is part of the sale from exiting it. Punishment was called 'hadd' because of the prevention it contains from committing obscenities. From it is the mourning ('ihdād) of a woman during her waiting period because she refrains from perfume and adornment. As for the usuliyyin, their definitions of 'hadd' varied. Al-Baqillani said: The boundary of every bounded thing is the explanation of the description of what is asked about and its name, by a statement that is comprehensive of the bounded and prevents anything not from it from entering it, or prevents anything that is from it from exiting it, and its hearer becomes closer upon hearing it to knowing the meaning of what was asked about. Al-Shirazi transmitted another definition from al-Baqillani, saying: The correct expression for the boundary is the expression of al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani: it is the expression about the intended meaning by what encompasses it and surrounds it with a surrounding that prevents what is not from it from entering it and prevents what is from it from exiting it. Al-Baji said: 'The boundary is the comprehensive and preventive expression.' Abu Muzaffar al-Sam'ani said: It is the concise expression that encompasses the meaning. Abu Ya'la al-Farra' said: The meaning of the boundary is that which gathers the species that detail dispersed, preventing what is not from its entirety from entering ... And the increase in the boundary is a deficiency in the bounded, because when the boundary gathers entities that were separate in the state of detail, if an excess amount is added to the mentioned, some of the entities exit from the entirety of the statement, so the boundary becomes for some after it was for all. Abu Bakr al-Baqillani said: The increase in it is of two types: one is a deficiency in it, and one is not a deficiency. As for that which is a deficiency, like your saying: The boundary of the obligatory (wajib) is that there is reward in doing it and punishment in abandoning it. This necessitates the exit of everything that is not fasting from being obligatory, so it returns as deficiency. As for that which is not a deficiency, like your saying: The boundary of the obligatory is that it is an obligation (fard); there is reward in doing it and punishment in abandoning it, for every obligation is obligatory. As for the deficiency from the boundary, it is always an increase in it, like your saying: The boundary of the obligatory is what has reward in doing it, without coupling punishment in abandoning it, so the supererogatory (nafl) enters into the category of the obligatory, because it is something for which one is rewarded.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the concept of 'hadd' (definition/boundary) as understood by usuliyyin (scholars of Islamic jurisprudence). It begins with the linguistic meaning—'prevention'—illustrated through examples: the doorkeeper, iron, boundaries of property, legal punishments, and the mourning period. These examples show that the root meaning of 'hadd' carries the idea of demarcation and restriction. The passage then presents multiple terminological definitions from prominent usuliyyin. Al-Baqillani's first definition emphasizes a statement that comprehensively gathers the defined thing and prevents anything extraneous from entering or anything essential from exiting. His second definition, transmitted by al-Shirazi, stresses encompassing and surrounding the intended meaning. Al-Baji offers the concise phrase 'the comprehensive and preventive expression' (al-lafdh al-jāmi' al-māni'). Al-Sam'ani adds the requirement of conciseness (al-wajīz). Abu Ya'la al-Farra' focuses on gathering the dispersed species and preventing entry of what is not from the whole. This leads to a discussion on the effect of increase in wording. Abu Ya'la states that any increase in the definition causes a deficiency in coverage because when the definition originally covered all entities, adding more words excludes some. Al-Baqillani distinguishes two types of increase: one that is a deficiency (like defining 'wajib' as 'doing it brings reward, abandoning it brings punishment' excludes acts like fasting, which are also wajib but not specified) and one that is not a deficiency (like defining 'wajib' as 'fard', since every fard is wajib). He also notes that a deficiency in the definition (e.g., omitting punishment for abandonment) is actually an increase that includes non-obligatory acts (like supererogatory deeds). The overall aim is to establish that a proper definition must be both inclusive of all instances and exclusive of non-instances, and that wording must be carefully chosen to avoid unintentional narrowing or broadening.

Key Terms
الزيادة في الحد
النقصان في المحدود

Checkpoint
According to al-Baqillani, which of the following is an example of an increase that is a deficiency?
A) Defining 'wajib' as 'fard'
B) Defining 'wajib' as 'what has reward in doing and punishment in leaving'
C) Defining 'wajib' as 'what has reward without mention of punishment'
D) Defining 'wajib' as 'what is commanded'

Key Takeaway
In definitions, more words can mean less coverage; omitting essential parts can expand coverage unintentionally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition of 'zakat' as 'obligatory charity on specific wealth.' According to al-Baqillani's analysis, what would happen if we added 'on livestock, produce, and money'? Would that increase cause a deficiency? Explain in one sentence.

Hadd and Rasm
beginner

Understanding the Definition of Ta'rīf
Author explains the linguistic origin of 'ta'rif' and its terminological definition as a statement that makes a concept known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the linguistic and terminological meanings of ta'rīf and identify its two purposes: conception of essence or complete distinction.

Core Idea
Definition (ta'rīf) is a statement that either reveals the essence of a concept or marks it off from everything else.

In this lesson, we examine how the term 'ta'rīf' itself is defined. The author starts with the linguistic origin—definition as the act of making known—then explains the terminological shift: it becomes the statement used to make something known. The key is that a proper definition in logic must serve one of two functions: giving a conception of the essence (kumh wa haqīqah) or providing a complete distinction (tamyīz kāmil) from all other things. Understanding this foundation is essential before learning about hadd and rasm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic meaning of ta'rīf
In the original language, ta'rīf is the act of the definer (fi'l al-mu'arraf). Note that it is a process, not a statement.
2
Trace the metaphorical extension to terminology
The passage explains that ta'rīf came to be used for the expression itself (lafz) by which something is defined. This is a metaphor because the expression is the effect of the definer's act.
3
Extract the terminological definition
In terminology, ta'rīf is a statement (qawl) that explains a single conceptual term (mufrad), whether universal or particular, to benefit the addressee.
4
Distinguish the two purposes of definition
The statement aims either to give a conception (tasawwur) of the term in its essence and reality, or to provide a complete distinction (tamyīz) from others. These correspond to hadd (essential definition) and rasm (descriptive definition) respectively.
5
Relate to al-Toufi's restatement
Al-Toufi rephrases: definition makes a thing known by what distinguishes it from what resembles it, mentioning its genus. This emphasizes the role of genus in distinction.
The full definition typically also requires the differentia (fasl), which may continue after the cut-off.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التعريف لغة واصطلاحا
حقيقة التعريف – في أصل اللغة – هو فعل المعرف، ثم أطلق في الاصطلاح على اللفظ المعرف به مجازا ، لأنه أثر اللافظ، كما أن التعريف أثر المعرف. أما في الاصطلاح، فالتعريف قول يشرح به مفرد من المفردات التصورية الكلية أو الجزئية لإفادة المخاطب تصور هذا المفرد بكنهه وحقيقته، أو لإفادته تمييزه عما عداه تمييزا (كاملا). قال الطوفي : «التعريف : هو تصيير الشيء معروفًا بما يميزه عما يشتبه به، بذكر جنسه
Definition (al-ta'rīf) linguistically and terminologically. The reality of definition – in the origin of language – is the act of the definer. Then it was used terminologically for the expression by which something is defined, metaphorically, because it is the effect of the speaker, just as the definition is the effect of the definer. As for terminology, definition is a statement by which a single conceptual term (mufrad) – universal or particular – is explained to benefit the addressee a conception (tasawwur) of that term in its essence and reality, or to benefit him a distinction from others by a (complete) distinction. Al-Toufi said: 'Definition is the making of a thing known by what distinguishes it from what resembles it, by mentioning its genus...'
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage defines 'al-ta'rīf' (definition) itself, first by its linguistic origin and then terminologically. Linguistically, it is the act of making known (fi'l al-mu'arraf). Terminologically, it is a statement (qawl) that explains a single conceptual term (mufrad) – whether universal (kullī) or particular (juz'ī) – in order to benefit the hearer a conception (tasawwur) of that term in its essence (kumh) and reality (haqīqah), or to benefit him a complete distinction (tamyīz) from others. The passage notes the metaphorical extension from the act to the expression used. It then quotes al-Toufi, who restates definition as the making of a thing known by what distinguishes it from what resembles it, by mentioning its genus (jins). The cut-off suggests the continuation would mention the differentia (fasl). This passage establishes that the purpose of definition is either to reveal the essence or to distinguish the defined from everything else, aligning with the classical logical notion of hadd (essential definition) and rasm (descriptive definition).

التعريف
المفرد
تصور
كنه
تمييز

Key Takeaway
The terminological definition of ta'rīf is a statement that explains a single concept to give either its essence or a complete distinction from everything else.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Principle of Definition: Increase is Decrease in the Defined
Abu Ya'la al-Farra' defines hadd and al-Baqillani distinguishes two types of increase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether an addition to a definition (hadd) causes deficiency in the defined (mahdud) using al-Baqillani's two types.

Core Idea
Adding to a definition can actually make it narrower, excluding what it should include.

This passage from Abu Ya'la al-Farra' and al-Baqillani teaches a critical lesson about definitions: that adding extra conditions can unintentionally exclude valid instances. Understanding this principle is essential for precise legal and theological definitions in Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Farra's definition of hadd
The hadd is 'the collector of the genus that the detail separates, preventing entry of what is not from its entirety.' This means a definition must include all and only the defined items.
2
Identify the principle of increase as deficiency
Al-Farra' states: 'Increase in the definition is a deficiency in the defined.' Adding extra wording excludes some essences that should be included.
3
Distinguish al-Baqillani's two types of increase
One type causes deficiency (e.g., defining wajib by reward and punishment excludes fasting because fasting has no punishment). The other type does not (e.g., adding 'fard' which is synonymous with wajib).
4
Recognize that deficiency always comes from increase
A deficiency in the definition always results from an increase in wording, such as adding a condition that excludes some items.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو يعلى الفراء : معنى الحد : هو الجامع لجنس ما فرقه التفصيل المانع من دخول ما ليس من جملته. والزيادة في الحد نقصان في المحدود لأن الحد متى جمع ذواتا كانت متفرقة حال التفصيل، فمتى ضم إليه قدر زائد على المذكور خرج بعض الذوات من جملة الكلام فيكون الحد للبعض بعد أن كان للجميع. وقال أبو بكر الباقلاني : الزيادة فيه على ضربين منها ما هو نقصان منه، ومنها ما هو ليس بنقصان . فأما التي هي نقصان نحو قولك : حد الواجب أنه في فعله ثواب، وفي تركه عقاب، فهذا يوجب خروج كل ما ليس بصيام عن كونه واجبا، فعادت بالنقصان . وأما ما ليس بنقصان نحو قولك : حد الواجب ؛ أنه فرض في فعله ثواب وفي تركه عقاب، فكل فرض واجب . وأما النقصان من الحد، فإنه - أبدا - زيادة فيه نحو قولك : حد الواجب ما كان في فعله ثواب، ولا يقرن به في تركه عقاب فيدخل النفل في جملة الواجب، لأنه مما يثاب عليه». (1)
Abu Ya'la al-Farra' said: The meaning of al-hadd (definition) is that which collects the genus of what the detail separates, preventing the entry of what is not from its entirety. And the increase in the definition is a deficiency in the defined, because when the definition collects essences that were separated in the state of detail, then when an amount greater than what was mentioned is added to it, some of the essences exit from the entirety of the speech, so the definition becomes for some after it was for all. And Abu Bakr al-Baqillani said: The increase in it is of two types: one of them is a deficiency from it, and one of them is not a deficiency. As for that which is a deficiency, like your saying: The definition of the obligatory (wajib) is that in doing it there is reward and in abandoning it there is punishment; this necessitates the exit of everything that is not fasting from being obligatory, so it returns with deficiency. And as for that which is not a deficiency, like your saying: The definition of the obligatory is that it is a prescribed duty (fard) such that in doing it there is reward and in abandoning it there is punishment; for every fard is obligatory. And as for the deficiency from the definition, it is always an increase in it, like your saying: The definition of the obligatory is that in doing it there is reward, and it is not paired with punishment in its abandonment; then supererogatory acts (nafl) enter into the category of the obligatory, because it is something for which one is rewarded.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of hadd (definition) and the principle that any addition to a proper definition results in a deficiency in the defined. It begins with Abu Ya'la al-Farra' stating that the hadd is what collects the genus that the detail separates, preventing entry of what is not from its category. Then he explains that if you add an extra element to the definition, some of the defined items are excluded, so the definition only covers a subset. Then al-Baqillani classifies increase into two types: one that is a deficiency (like defining 'obligatory' solely by reward and punishment, which excludes fasting since it has no punishment) and one that is not a deficiency (like adding 'fard' to the definition, which is synonymous with wajib). He also notes that a deficiency in the definition is always an increase in its wording (like adding a condition that excludes some items). The examples use Islamic legal categories: wajib (obligatory), fard (prescribed duty), nafl (supererogatory).

الزيادة
النقصان
الواجب

A) Adding 'punishment for abandonment' to the definition of wajib.
B) Adding 'fard' to the definition of wajib.
C) Adding 'reward for doing it' to the definition of nafl.

Key Takeaway
The principle that increase in a definition can cause deficiency is crucial for crafting precise definitions in Islamic legal theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the definition of 'wajib' as 'that in doing which there is reward and in abandoning which there is punishment'. Check whether this definition includes fasting (sawm) as a valid instance. According to al-Baqillani, it does not, because fasting has no punishment for abandonment. So it is a deficient definition.
?

The Definition of Ta'rif
Al-Tufi, al-Jurjani, and al-Kafawi define ta'rif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a ta'rif and distinguish its three types according to al-Tufi.

Core Idea
Ta'rif is broader than you think: essence, property, or synonym.

This lesson unpacks the term 'ta'rif' (definition/description) as used by scholars. We start with its linguistic origin, then explore the terminological definitions given by al-Tufi, al-Jurjani, and al-Kafawi, each adding a layer to our understanding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic origin
Note that ta'rif originally means the act of making known, then metaphorically transferred to the word that does the defining.
This shift from action to instrument is common in Arabic terminology.
2
Extract the terminological definition
The passage gives a general definition: ta'rif is a statement explaining a conceptual term to give conception or distinction. Underline that it aims at either essence (kannah) or distinction (tamyiz).
This is the core definition; the scholars expand on it.
3
Analyze al-Tufi's three methods
Al-Tufi says ta'rif works by (a) genus + differentia, (b) a necessary concomitant (lazim), or (c) explaining a strange word with a familiar one. List these and think of examples.
This shows ta'rif is not limited to essential definition.
4
Compare with al-Jurjani and al-Kafawi
Al-Jurjani focuses on knowledge leading to another knowledge; al-Kafawi includes quiddity and distinction. How do they overlap with al-Tufi?
They all agree ta'rif clarifies, but differ in scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التعريف لغة واصطلاحا
حقيقة التعريف – في أصل اللغة - هو فعل المعرف، ثم أطلق في الاصطلاح على اللفظ المعرف به مجازا ، لأنه أثر اللافظ، كما أن التعريف أثر المعرف(2) . أما في الاصطلاح، فالتعريف قول يشرح به مفرد من المفردات التصورية الكلية أو الجزئية لإفادة المخاطب تصور هذا المفرد بكنهه وحقيقته، أو لإفادته تمييزه عما عداه تمييزا (كاملا) (3) .
قال الطوفي : «التعريف : هو تصيير الشيء معروفًا بما يميزه عما يشتبه به، بذكر جنسه وفصله أو لازم من لوازمه التي لا توجد في غيره، أو شرح اللفظ الغريب بلفظ مشهور مألوف»(1).
وقال الجرجاني : التعريف عبارة عن ذكر شيء تستلزم معرفته معرفة شيء آخره (2) .
وقال الكفوي : التعريف : هو إعلام ماهية الشيء أو ما يميزه عن الغير فالتعريف بالشيء - إذا - هو الكشف عن خصائصه وبيان ما يتميز به عن غيره، أو هو شرح لفظ غريب بلفظ مرادف أكثر شهرة ووضوحا. ج - التفريق بين الحد والتعريف: ذلك هو مفهوم كل من الحد والتعريف فهل هناك فرق بينهما أو تمايز في حقيقتهما؟ . . يقول الإمام نجم الدين الطوفي رحمه الله في هذا الصدد: والتعريف أعم من الحد، لأن التعريف يحصل بذكر لازم، أو خاصة، أو لفظ، يحصل معه الاطراد والانعكاس، والحد لا يحصل إلا بذكر الجنس والفصل المتضمن لجميع ذاتيات المحدود، فكل حد تعريف، وليس كل تعريف حدا، لأنه قد لا يتضمن جميع الذاتيات»(4).
وهذا تفريق في الحقيقة متأثر بنظرة المناطقة إلى كل من التعريف والحد، أما الأصوليون، والمتقدمون منهم خاصة، فلا يفرقون بينهما، إذ أن كلا والتعريف إنما يراد منهما تمييز المحدود أو المعرف عن غيره بوصف يخصه . وفي ذلك يقول الفاكهاني (5) : اعلم أن الحد والمعرف - في عرف النحاة والفقهاء والأصوليين - اسمان لمسمى، وهو ما يميز الشيء عن جميع ما عداه»(6)
Al-Ta'rif linguistically and terminologically. The reality of al-ta'rif – in the origin of the language – is the act of the definer, then it was used terminologically for the word by which something is defined, metaphorically, because it is the effect of the speaker, just as ta'rif is the effect of the definer. As for terminologically, al-ta'rif is a statement by which a single term, among conceptual universals or particulars, is explained to benefit the addressee a conception of this term in its essence and reality, or to benefit him a (complete) distinction of it from others. Al-Tufi said: 'Al-ta'rif is making a thing known by what distinguishes it from what resembles it, by mentioning its genus and differentia, or a necessary concomitant among its concomitants that does not exist in other than it, or explaining a strange word with a familiar well-known word.' Al-Jurjani said: 'Al-ta'rif is an expression of mentioning something whose knowledge necessitates knowledge of something else.' Al-Kafawi said: 'Al-ta'rif is informing of the quiddity of a thing or what distinguishes it from others; so al-ta'rif of a thing is revealing its characteristics and explaining what it is distinguished by from others, or it is explaining a strange word with a synonymous word that is more famous and clear.' Section: Distinction between al-hadd and al-ta'rif: That is the concept of each of al-hadd and al-ta'rif – is there a difference between them or a distinction in their reality? ... Imam Najm al-Din al-Tufi, may Allah have mercy on him, said in this regard: 'Al-ta'rif is more general than al-hadd, because al-ta'rif is obtained by mentioning a concomitant, or a property, or a word with which regularity and reversal are obtained, while al-hadd is not obtained except by mentioning the genus and differentia that encompass all the essentials of the defined. So every hadd is a ta'rif, but not every ta'rif is a hadd, because it may not encompass all the essentials.' This distinction in reality is influenced by the view of the logicians regarding both al-ta'rif and al-hadd. As for the usuliyyun (legal theorists), especially the early ones, they do not distinguish between them, since both hadd and ta'rif are intended only to distinguish the defined or the described from others by a characteristic that is specific to it. And in this regard, al-Fakahani said: 'Know that al-hadd and al-mu'arraf (the definer) – in the terminology of the grammarians, jurists, and usuliyyun – are two names for one referent, which is what distinguishes a thing from all others.'
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive treatment of al-ta'rif (definition/description) terminologically and its relation to al-hadd. It begins by explaining the linguistic origin of ta'rif as the act of making known, then its terminological use as the word that defines. The definition given is that ta'rif is a statement (qawl) that explains a single conceptual term, universal or particular, to give the listener a conception of its essence or at least a distinction from others. Then it quotes three scholars: al-Tufi defines ta'rif as making a thing known by its genus and differentia, or by a necessary property, or by paraphrasing a strange word. Al-Jurjani defines it as mentioning something whose knowledge leads to knowledge of another thing. Al-Kafawi defines it as informing of the quiddity or the distinguishing feature, or as synonym explanation. Then a section on the distinction between hadd and ta'rif. Al-Tufi says ta'rif is more general (a'mm) because it can be done by a necessary property or a word, while hadd requires genus and differentia covering all essentials. Therefore every hadd is a ta'rif, but not vice versa. The passage then notes that this view is influenced by the logicians (mantiqiyyun). In contrast, the early usuliyyun (like al-Fakahani) did not distinguish between them; for them both hadd and ta'rif are simply what distinguishes a thing from everything else. This reflects a difference in approach: the logicians emphasize the essential components (genus and differentia) for a proper definition, while the legal theorists focus on the function of distinguishing.

Distinction Between Hadd and Ta'rif
Al-Tufi and al-Fakahani on the relationship between hadd and ta'rif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between hadd (logical definition) and ta'rif (broader description) according to al-Tufi and contrast it with the early usuli view.

Core Idea
Every hadd is a ta'rif, but not every ta'rif is a hadd.

While the previous lesson defined ta'rif broadly, this one sharpens the distinction between ta'rif and a more specific term: hadd. Al-Tufi argues ta'rif is more general, while early usuliyyun like al-Fakahani treated them as synonyms. Let's see the logical and practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Tufi's main claim
Al-Tufi states: 'والتعريف أعم من الحد' (ta'rif is more general than hadd). Underline this sentence.
This is the key logical relation.
2
Understand why ta'rif is more general
Al-Tufi explains: ta'rif can be achieved by mentioning a necessary concomitant (lazim), a specific property, or a word that gives regularity and reversal. Hadd, however, requires mentioning the genus and differentia that encompass all the essentials (dhatiyyat) of the defined.
Thus hadd is a subtype of ta'rif that uses jins+fasl and covers all essentials.
3
Contrast with the early usuli view
The passage notes that early usuliyyun (like al-Fakahani) did not distinguish: for them, both hadd and ta'rif are simply 'what distinguishes a thing from all others'. Al-Fakahani says in the terminology of grammarians, jurists, and usuliyyun, they are synonyms.
This reflects a difference in precision: logicians vs. legal theorists.
4
Identify the influence of logicians
The passage explicitly says al-Tufi's distinction is influenced by the logicians (al-mantiqiyyun). This shows how logical theory entered usul al-fiqh.
This is an example of the integration of logic into Islamic sciences.

Al-Ta'rif linguistically and terminologically. Defining Ta'rif: Three Classical Views
Author defines ta'rif, compares hadd and ta'rif, and contrasts usuli with Greek logician views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the three definitions of ta'rif given by al-Tufi, al-Jurjani, and al-Kafawi and identify the common criterion of distinguishing the defined.

Core Idea
Ta'rif unifies revealing quiddity and distinguishing the defined.

This lesson examines three classical definitions of ta'rif (definition) from leading scholars. Each definition, while phrased differently, converges on the idea that ta'rif makes something known by revealing its characteristics or by clarifying what makes it unique. By comparing them, you will grasp the essential function of definitions in Islamic scholarly discourse.

Guided Path
1
Extract each definition
Read the Arabic passage and find the definitions attributed to al-Tufi, al-Jurjani, and al-Kafawi. Note the key phrases each uses.
Al-Tufi mentions 'differentia, necessary property, or explaining an unfamiliar term'; al-Jurjani says 'mentioning something whose knowledge entails knowledge of another'; al-Kafawi says 'informing of quiddity or what distinguishes it from others'.
2
Identify the common core
Compare the three definitions. What do they all aim to do? They all seek to make the defined thing known and distinguished from others.
Despite different wording, the common function is 'distinguishing the defined from others' (tamyiz).
3
Note the differences in scope
Observe that al-Tufi's definition includes both essential (differentia) and non-essential (necessary property) elements, while al-Jurjani's is more abstract, and al-Kafawi's explicitly mentions quiddity.
This range shows ta'rif can be broader than a strict logical hadd.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفصله أو لازم من لوازمه التي لا توجد في غيره، أو شرح اللفظ الغريب بلفظ مشهور مألوف»(1). وقال الجرجاني : التعريف عبارة عن ذكر شيء تستلزم معرفته معرفة شيء آخره (2) . (3) وقال الكفوي : التعريف : هو إعلام ماهية الشيء أو ما يميزه عن الغير فالتعريف بالشيء - إذا - هو الكشف عن خصائصه وبيان ما يتميز به عن غيره، أو هو شرح لفظ غريب بلفظ مرادف أكثر شهرة ووضوحا. ج - التفريق بين الحد والتعريف: ذلك هو مفهوم كل من الحد والتعريف فهل هناك فرق بينهما أو تمايز في حقيقتهما؟ . . يقول الإمام نجم الدين الطوفي رحمه الله في هذا الصدد: والتعريف أعم من الحد، لأن التعريف يحصل بذكر لازم، أو خاصة، أو لفظ، يحصل معه الاطراد والانعكاس، والحد لا يحصل إلا بذكر الجنس والفصل المتضمن لجميع ذاتيات المحدود، فكل حد تعريف، وليس كل تعريف حدا، لأنه قد لا يتضمن جميع الذاتيات»(4). وهذا تفريق في الحقيقة متأثر بنظرة المناطقة إلى كل من التعريف والحد، أما الأصوليون، والمتقدمون منهم خاصة، فلا يفرقون بينهما، إذ أن كلا والتعريف إنما يراد منهما تمييز المحدود أو المعرف عن غيره بوصف يخصه . وفي ذلك يقول الفاكهاني (3) : اعلم أن الحد والمعرف - في عرف النحاة والفقهاء والأصوليين - اسمان لمسمى، وهو ما يميز الشيء عن جميع ما عداه»(6) وعلماء الشريعة الإسلامية في هذا يخالفون أهل المنطق اليوناني ومن حذا حذوهم من الفلاسفة الإسلاميين الذين يقولون إن القصد من الحد هو تصوير المحدود وتعريف حقيقته، في حين اكتفى علماء الشريعة الإسلامية بأن الحد هو تمييز المحدود عن غيره فثبت - إذا - أن الحد والتعريف بمعنى واحد غاية ما هنالك أن القدامى من الأصوليين وحتى المتأخرين منهم جروا على إطلاق لفظ الحد على التعريف، في أن المحدثين والمعاصرين أصبحوا يفضلون إطلاق مصطلح التعريف بدلا من مصطلح الحد.
And its differentia or one of its necessary properties that is not found in another, or explaining an unfamiliar term with a well-known familiar expression. Al-Jurjani said: 'The definition is an expression for mentioning something whose knowledge necessarily entails knowledge of something else.' Al-Kafawi said: 'The definition is informing of the quiddity of a thing or what distinguishes it from others; so definition of a thing is revealing its characteristics and clarifying what it is distinguished by from others, or it is explaining an unfamiliar term with a synonymous term that is more famous and clear.' J – The distinction between hadd and ta'rif: That is the concept of each of hadd and ta'rif. Is there a difference between them or a distinction in their essence? ... Imam Najm al-Din al-Tufi, may Allah have mercy on him, said in this regard: 'And ta'rif is more general than hadd, because ta'rif is attained by mentioning a necessary property, or a specific property, or a term by which coextensiveness and converseness are attained, while hadd is not attained except by mentioning the genus and differentia that encompass all the essential properties of the limited; so every hadd is a ta'rif, and not every ta'rif is a hadd, because it may not encompass all the essentialities.' This distinction in reality is influenced by the logicians' view of each of ta'rif and hadd. As for the usuliyyun, especially the early ones among them, they do not distinguish between them, because both (hadd and ta'rif) are intended only to distinguish the limited or defined thing from others by a property specific to it. In that regard, al-Fakahani said: 'Know that hadd and mu'arraf – in the usage of grammarians, jurists, and usuliyyun – are two names for one referent, which is what distinguishes the thing from everything else.' And the scholars of Islamic law in this oppose the Greek logicians and those who follow them among the Islamic philosophers who say that the purpose of hadd is to depict the limited and define its reality, whereas the scholars of Islamic law sufficed with the fact that hadd is to distinguish the limited from others. So it is established that hadd and ta'rif have the same meaning, except that the early usuliyyun and even the later ones continued to use the term hadd for ta'rif, while modern and contemporary ones have preferred to use the term ta'rif instead of hadd.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage begins by presenting three definitions of 'ta'rif' from classical scholars: al-Tufi defines it as mentioning a differentia or necessary property or explaining an unfamiliar term; al-Jurjani defines it as something whose knowledge entails knowledge of another thing; al-Kafawi defines it as revealing the quiddity or distinguishing characteristics. Then the author introduces the central question of the difference between 'hadd' and 'ta'rif' (the distinction). The author cites al-Tufi's view that ta'rif is broader than hadd because ta'rif can be done through a necessary property (lazim) or a specific property (khassa) or a term that ensures coextensiveness and converse, while hadd requires genus (jins) and differentia (fasl) that include all essential properties (dhatiyyat) of the defined. Hence every hadd is a ta'rif but not vice versa. However, the author notes that this distinction is influenced by a logical perspective. The early usuliyyun (Islamic legal theorists) do not distinguish between them; for them both aim solely at distinguishing the defined from others by a property unique to it. Al-Fakahani confirms this, stating that in the terminology of grammarians, jurists, and usuliyyun, 'hadd' and 'mu'arraf' are synonymous. The author then explains that Islamic legal scholars oppose Greek logicians and their Islamic followers, who argue that hadd aims to depict the reality (taswir al-mahdud) and define its essence, whereas Islamic legal scholars suffice with distinguishing the defined from others. Consequently, hadd and ta'rif are synonymous in their usage, though modern scholars tend to prefer the term 'ta'rif'. The passage thus clarifies a terminological shift and underscores the practical orientation of Islamic scholars toward definitions.

الخاصة

Hadd vs Ta'rif: Logicians vs Usuliyyun
Author defines ta'rif, compares hadd and ta'rif, and contrasts usuli with Greek logician views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the logicians' hierarchical view of hadd and ta'rif and the usuli view that they are synonyms, and explain the practical reason for the divergence.

Core Idea
For usuliyyun, definitions are practical tools; for logicians, they are essential depictions.

This lesson delves into the key distinction between 'hadd' and 'ta'rif' as presented by al-Tufi and contrasts it with the usuli perspective. You will see that while logicians see hadd as a precise essential definition and ta'rif as broader, early Islamic legal theorists treat them as interchangeable, focused solely on distinguishing the defined. This reflects different intellectual aims: logical completeness versus legal utility.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Tufi's distinction
Read al-Tufi's statement: 'Ta'rif is more general than hadd... every hadd is a ta'rif, not every ta'rif is a hadd.' Identify why he says this: ta'rif can use necessary or specific properties, while hadd requires genus, differentia, and all essential properties.
This is the logician-influenced view.
2
Identify the core difference in purpose
Why did logicians require all essentialities for hadd? Because their goal was 'taswir al-mahdud' (depicting the reality). For usuliyyun, the goal is merely 'tamyiz' (distinguishing). Understand this difference in intention.
Greek logicians aimed at essential definitions; Islamic scholars aimed at practical identification.
3
Contrast with the usuli view
Read al-Fakahani's statement: 'Hadd and mu'arraf are two names for one referent – what distinguishes the thing from everything else.' Note that for grammarians, jurists, and usuliyyun, they are synonyms.
This view ignores the logicians' hierarchy.
4
Recognize the historical terminological shift
The author concludes that early usuliyyun used 'hadd' for all definitions, but modern scholars prefer 'ta'rif'. Consider why: 'ta'rif' is broader and avoids confusion with the stricter logical hadd.
This shows how disciplines adapt terminology to their needs.

Source Text

وفصله أو لازم من لوازمه التي لا توجد في غيره، أو شرح اللفظ الغريب بلفظ مشهور مألوف»(1). وقال الجرجاني : التعريف عبارة عن ذكر شيء تستلزم معرفته معرفة شيء آخره (2) . (3) وقال الكفوي : التعريف : هو إعلام ماهية الشيء أو ما يميزه عن الغير فالتعريف بالشيء - إذا - هو الكشف عن خصائصه وبيان ما يتميز به عن غيره، أو هو شرح لفظ غريب بلفظ مرادف أكثر شهرة ووضوحا. ج - التفريق بين الحد والتعريف: ذلك هو مفهوم كل من الحد والتعريف فهل هناك فرق بينهما أو تمايز في حقيقتهما؟ . . يقول الإمام نجم الدين الطوفي رحمه الله في هذا الصدد: والتعريف أعم من الحد، لأن التعريف يحصل بذكر لازم، أو خاصة، أو لفظ، يحصل معه الاطراد والانعكاس، والحد لا يحصل إلا بذكر الجنس والفصل المتضمن لجميع ذاتيات المحدود، فكل حد تعريف، وليس كل تعريف حدا، لأنه قد لا يتضمن جميع الذاتيات»(4). وهذا تفريق في الحقيقة متأثر بنظرة المناطقة إلى كل من التعريف والحد، أما الأصوليون، والمتقدمون منهم خاصة، فلا يفرقون بينهما، إذ أن كلا والتعريف إنما يراد منهما تمييز المحدود أو المعرف عن غيره بوصف يخصه . وفي ذلك يقول الفاكهاني (3) : اعلم أن الحد والمعرف - في عرف النحاة والفقهاء والأصوليين - اسمان لمسمى، وهو ما يميز الشيء عن جميع ما عداه»(6) وعلماء الشريعة الإسلامية في هذا يخالفون أهل المنطق اليوناني ومن حذا حذوهم من الفلاسفة الإسلاميين الذين يقولون إن القصد من الحد هو تصوير المحدود وتعريف حقيقته، في حين اكتفى علماء الشريعة الإسلامية بأن الحد هو تمييز المحدود عن غيره فثبت - إذا - أن الحد والتعريف بمعنى واحد غاية ما هنالك أن القدامى من الأصوليين وحتى المتأخرين منهم جروا على إطلاق لفظ الحد على التعريف، في أن المحدثين والمعاصرين أصبحوا يفضلون إطلاق مصطلح التعريف بدلا من مصطلح الحد.
And its differentia or one of its necessary properties that is not found in another, or explaining an unfamiliar term with a well-known familiar expression. Al-Jurjani said: 'The definition is an expression for mentioning something whose knowledge necessarily entails knowledge of something else.' Al-Kafawi said: 'The definition is informing of the quiddity of a thing or what distinguishes it from others; so definition of a thing is revealing its characteristics and clarifying what it is distinguished by from others, or it is explaining an unfamiliar term with a synonymous term that is more famous and clear.' J – The distinction between hadd and ta'rif: That is the concept of each of hadd and ta'rif. Is there a difference between them or a distinction in their essence? ... Imam Najm al-Din al-Tufi, may Allah have mercy on him, said in this regard: 'And ta'rif is more general than hadd, because ta'rif is attained by mentioning a necessary property, or a specific property, or a term by which coextensiveness and converseness are attained, while hadd is not attained except by mentioning the genus and differentia that encompass all the essential properties of the limited; so every hadd is a ta'rif, and not every ta'rif is a hadd, because it may not encompass all the essentialities.' This distinction in reality is influenced by the logicians' view of each of ta'rif and hadd. As for the usuliyyun, especially the early ones among them, they do not distinguish between them, because both (hadd and ta'rif) are intended only to distinguish the limited or defined thing from others by a property specific to it. In that regard, al-Fakahani said: 'Know that hadd and mu'arraf – in the usage of grammarians, jurists, and usuliyyun – are two names for one referent, which is what distinguishes the thing from everything else.' And the scholars of Islamic law in this oppose the Greek logicians and those who follow them among the Islamic philosophers who say that the purpose of hadd is to depict the limited and define its reality, whereas the scholars of Islamic law sufficed with the fact that hadd is to distinguish the limited from others. So it is established that hadd and ta'rif have the same meaning, except that the early usuliyyun and even the later ones continued to use the term hadd for ta'rif, while modern and contemporary ones have preferred to use the term ta'rif instead of hadd.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage begins by presenting three definitions of 'ta'rif' from classical scholars: al-Tufi defines it as mentioning a differentia or necessary property or explaining an unfamiliar term; al-Jurjani defines it as something whose knowledge entails knowledge of another thing; al-Kafawi defines it as revealing the quiddity or distinguishing characteristics. Then the author introduces the central question of the difference between 'hadd' and 'ta'rif' (the distinction). The author cites al-Tufi's view that ta'rif is broader than hadd because ta'rif can be done through a necessary property (lazim) or a specific property (khassa) or a term that ensures coextensiveness and converse, while hadd requires genus (jins) and differentia (fasl) that include all essential properties (dhatiyyat) of the defined. Hence every hadd is a ta'rif but not vice versa. However, the author notes that this distinction is influenced by a logical perspective. The early usuliyyun (Islamic legal theorists) do not distinguish between them; for them both aim solely at distinguishing the defined from others by a property unique to it. Al-Fakahani confirms this, stating that in the terminology of grammarians, jurists, and usuliyyun, 'hadd' and 'mu'arraf' are synonymous. The author then explains that Islamic legal scholars oppose Greek logicians and their Islamic followers, who argue that hadd aims to depict the reality (taswir al-mahdud) and define its essence, whereas Islamic legal scholars suffice with distinguishing the defined from others. Consequently, hadd and ta'rif are synonymous in their usage, though modern scholars tend to prefer the term 'ta'rif'. The passage thus clarifies a terminological shift and underscores the practical orientation of Islamic scholars toward definitions.

التعريف
اللازم
الخاصة
الجنس

Avoiding Defects in Definitions According to Ibn 'Aqil
Lists prohibitions in defining: ambiguity, redundancy, obscurity, and using remote genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and avoid the four main defects (ambiguity, redundancy, obscurity, and using a remote genus) when constructing a definition in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
A valid definition is concise, precise, and uses the nearest genus — not the highest.

In this passage, Imam Ibn 'Aqil lays out the essential conditions for a sound definition (hadd) by listing the defects that must be avoided. A definition aims to clarify the meaning of a term, so anything that introduces ambiguity, redundancy, or unnecessary generality defeats its purpose. You will examine each defect with concrete examples from Islamic logic and learn how to apply these principles when defining terms in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of definition
Recall that the goal of a definition is clarification and elucidation (al-idah wa al-bayan). Any defect that obscures meaning or adds unnecessary elements violates this purpose.
2
Recognize the first defect: ambiguity (al-mushtarak)
Find the example: defining 'ilm (knowledge) as idrak (perception). Idrak is mushtarak – it can mean sense perception as well. Thus it includes too much, failing to delimit the term properly.
In usul al-fiqh, mushtarak terms like 'qur'' (menstruation or purity) require careful handling in definitions.
3
Recognize the second defect: redundancy (al-hashw)
Look for the example of defining human as 'the writer who wears a sword'. The part 'who wears a sword' is redundant; omitting it does not affect the definition. Such redundancy makes the definition imprecise.
Hashw is also discussed in Arabic rhetoric as a stylistic flaw.
4
Recognize the third defect: obscurity (al-ibham)
Note the example of vague phrases like 'and what runs this course' (wa ma jara hadha al-majra). Such expressions are unclear and do not specify the defining properties.
Clarity is paramount; a definition should not require further interpretation.
5
Recognize the fourth set: remote genus, longer expression, and more general term
Ibn 'Aqil prohibits using a higher (remote) genus when a lower (proximate) genus is available, e.g., defining human as 'substance' (jism) instead of 'living being' (hayy). Similarly, avoid longer expressions (e.g., 'walks on two legs') when a short one suffices, and avoid more general terms (e.g., 'body' instead of 'living being').
The proximate genus is closest to the species and provides the most precise classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني : شروط صحة الحد أو التعريف
إن الحد أو التعريف بما أن المراد منه هو الإيضاح والبيان والكشف عن معنى المصطلح فلابد ليتحقق فيه هذا المراد أن تتوفر فيه جملة من الشروط، وهذه الشروط تتوفر حينما يتخلص التعريف أو الحد من جملة من العيوب، هي التي يحذر منها الإمام ابن عقيل حين يقول : اعلم أنه لا يجوز أن تأتي في الحد بالمشترك، كقولك في العلم : إدراك، فيدخل فيه سائر درك الحواس . ولا بما لو أسقطته لم يختل الحد، لأنه هو الحشو، والحد خلاصة لا تحتمل الحشو، مع كونه مشروطا بإيجاز اللفظ ، وذلك مثل قولك في حد الإنسان : الكاتب المتقلد السيف، وفي العلم الذي لا يتطرق عليه شك ولا شبهة، فهذه زيادة في الحد تنقص المحدود، فخرج بعض الناس عن الحد، وتخرج بعض العلوم وهي الاستدلالية ويخص الحد بعلم الضرورة وعلم القديم سبحانه . . . ولا يجوز فيه الإبهام، مثل قولك : وما جرى هذا المجرى أو مجرى ذلك، وما كان كذلك، حتى تتبين من أي وجه يكون . ولا يجوز أن تأتي بالجنس الأعلى وأنت تقدر على الأدنى، مثل قولك في حد الإنسان جوهر أو جسم وأنت تقدر أن تقول : حي، ولا باللفظ الأطول وأنت تقدر على الأقصر، مثل قولك : يمشي على رجلين ويبطش باليدين، ولا بالأعم وأنت تقدر على الأخص مثل قولك جسم، وأنت تقدر على حي هذا
The Second Section: The Conditions for the Validity of a Definition (al-hadd or al-ta'rif)
Since the aim of a definition is clarification, explanation, and revealing the meaning of the term, it is necessary for this aim to be realized that a number of conditions are met. These conditions are met when the definition is free from a number of defects, which Imam Ibn 'Aqil warns against when he says: Know that it is not permitted to use in the definition an ambiguous term (al-mushtarak), like your saying in defining knowledge ('ilm): 'perception' (idrak), for this includes all the perceptions of the senses. Nor [is it permitted to use] something that if you omitted it the definition would not be defective, because that is redundancy (al-hashw), and a definition is a summary that does not tolerate redundancy, along with being conditioned by brevity of expression. That is like your saying in defining human: 'the writer who wears a sword', and [in defining] knowledge that is not affected by doubt or uncertainty; these are additions to the definition that diminish the defined, so some people are excluded from the definition, and some sciences are excluded, namely the inferential sciences, and the definition is restricted to necessary knowledge and the knowledge of the Eternal, Exalted is He. ... And it is not permitted to use obscurity (al-ibham), like your saying: 'and what runs this course or the course of that', and 'what is like that', until it becomes clear from which aspect it is. And it is not permitted to use the higher genus when you are able to use the lower, like your saying in defining human: 'substance' or 'body' when you are able to say 'living being' (hayy); nor [to use] the longer expression when you are able to use the shorter, like your saying: 'walks on two legs and strikes with hands'; nor [to use] the more general when you are able to use the more specific, like your saying 'body' when you are able to say 'living being'.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage outlines the conditions for a valid definition (hadd/ta'rif) by listing the defects it must avoid, quoting from Ibn 'Aqil. The first defect is using a mushtarak (homonym/ambiguous term), which would include more than intended, e.g., defining 'knowledge' as 'perception' which also covers sense perceptions. The second is hashw (redundancy), i.e., including a part whose omission does not harm the definition, like 'writer who wears a sword' for human—this adds superfluous characteristics and may exclude some humans. The third is ibham (obscurity), such as vague phrases like 'and what runs this course'. The fourth is using a higher (more remote) genus when a lower (proximate) genus is available, e.g., defining human as 'substance' or 'body' instead of 'living being'. Fifth is using a longer expression when a shorter one suffices, like describing human as 'walks on two legs and strikes with hands' rather than 'living being'. Sixth is using a more general term when a more specific one is available, e.g., 'body' instead of 'living being'. These defects undermine the purpose of definition: clear and precise delimitation of the defined.

Key Terms
المشترك
الحشو
الإبهام
الجنس الأعلى

C) Defining human as 'substance' instead of 'living being'

Key Takeaway
The four defects—ambiguity, redundancy, obscurity, and using a remote genus—undermine the clarity and precision of a definition, which is the very purpose of defining.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Condition of Coextensiveness in Definitions
Defines the condition that a definition must be coextensive with the defined term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to test whether a definition satisfies the condition of coextensiveness (al-ittiraad wa al-in'ikaas) by identifying violations of inclusiveness or exclusiveness.

Core Idea
A definition must fit its defined term perfectly, covering all and only its instances.

This passage from the usuliyyun introduces the first essential condition of a valid definition: perfect equality in extension. The definition must be neither broader, narrower, nor entirely different. We will learn to apply this test using the criteria of jami' mani' (comprehensive and exclusive).

Guided Path
1
Understand the two components of coextensiveness
Tard (forward): wherever the defined term applies, the definition must apply. 'Aks (converse): wherever the definition applies, the defined term must apply. Both must hold for a valid definition.
2
Identify the three types of violations
(a) A'amm (broader): definition covers things outside the defined. (b) Akhass (narrower): definition misses some instances of the defined. (c) Mubayin (different): definition is completely unrelated.
Each violation leads to a false conception.
3
Apply the test to a candidate definition
Given a definition and a defined term, check: are all instances of the defined covered by the definition? (test tard) Are all instances of the definition actually instances of the defined? (test 'aks).
Use the jami' mani' terminology: comprehensive (jami') corresponds to tard, exclusive (mani') corresponds to 'aks.
4
Evaluate using Al-Shirazi's and Al-Juwayni's remarks
Al-Shirazi's palm-to-palm metaphor emphasizes exact fit. Al-Juwayni's example ('thing' and 'existent') shows how tard and 'aks are both required in legal and rational definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد نص الأصوليون على ضرورة توفر الحد أو التعريف على جملة من الشروط الأساسية، وهي:
أ - الاطراد والانعكاس:
ومعناه ؛ أن يكون مساويًا تمامًا للمفرد الذي نشرحه فلا يصح أن يكون أعم منه ، ولا أن يكون أخص منه، ولا أن يكون مباينا له، لأنه إذا كان أعم فإننا نجعل المخاطب يدخل في تصوره جزئيات هي خارجة عن المفرد الذي نشرحه له، وإذا كان أخص منه فإننا نجعل المخاطب يخرج في تصوره جزئيات هي داخلة في المفرد الذي نشرحه له وإذا كان مباينا له فإننا نشرح الشيء بمغاير له تماما وبذلك نعطيه صورة غير صحيحة، ونوقعه في تصور خاطئ مخالف للواقع والحقيقة .
من كل هذا يتبين لنا أنه يشترط في التعريف أن يكون مساويا تماما للمفرد الذي نشرحه به، ويعبرون عن هذا الشرط بقولهم : يشترط في التعريف أن يكون جامعا مانعا وبقولهم: يشترط في التعريف أن يكون مطردًا منعكسا»
قال الشيرازي :
من شرط الحد أن يطرد وينعكس، فيوجد المحدود بوجوده، ويعدم بعدمه . ومتى لم يكن كذلك لم يكن حدًا، وينطبق على المحدود انطباق الكف على الكف لا يفضل أحدهما على الآخر .
وقال الجويني : من حكم الحد؛ أن يكون مشروطا بالعكس لا محالة شرع كان أو عقليّا، وهو أن تقول في حد الشيء : إنه الموجود، فطرده أن تقول : كل شيء موجود وعكسه أن تقول : وكل موجود شيء
And the usulists have stated the necessity that a definition (al-hadd or al-ta'rif) possess a number of essential conditions, which are:
A - Coextensiveness (al-ittiraad wa al-in'ikaas):
Its meaning is that it [the definition] must be perfectly equal to the individual term we are explaining. So it is not valid for it to be more general than it, nor more specific than it, nor completely different from it. Because if it is more general, then we cause the addressee to include in his conception particulars that are outside the individual term we are explaining to him. And if it is more specific, then we cause the addressee to exclude in his conception particulars that are inside the individual term we are explaining to him. And if it is completely different, then we explain the thing by something entirely other than it, and thereby we give him an incorrect image and cause him to have a false conception contrary to reality and truth.
From all this it becomes clear to us that it is required in a definition that it be perfectly equal to the individual term we are explaining. And they express this condition by saying: 'It is required in the definition that it be comprehensive (jami') and exclusive (mani')' and by saying: 'It is required in the definition that it be coextensive (mutarid mun'akis).'
Al-Shirazi said:
Among the conditions of a definition is that it be coextensive (yatrid) and reciprocally convertible (yan'akis), so that the defined exists with its existence and ceases with its non-existence. And whenever it is not like that, it is not a definition. And it applies to the defined like the application of the palm to the palm, neither exceeding the other.
And al-Juwayni said: Among the rulings of a definition is that it must necessarily be conditional on reciprocity, whether it is a legal or rational definition. That is, you say in the definition of a thing: it is existent. Its coextensiveness (tard) is that you say: 'Everything existent is a thing', and its converseness ('aks) is that you say: 'And everything thing is existent'.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents the first and most essential condition of a valid definition: coextensiveness (al-ittiraad wa al-in'ikaas), also known as 'comprehensive and exclusive' (jami' mani'). The author explains that the definition must be exactly equal in extension to the defined term. It must not be broader (a'amm), because then the addressee would include things outside the defined; nor narrower (akhass), because then they would exclude things that belong; nor utterly different (mubayin), because then they would have a completely wrong conception. The condition is illustrated by quotes: al-Shirazi compares it to the perfect fit of palm to palm, and al-Juwayni gives an example: if you define a thing as 'existent', then the tard (forward implication) is 'everything existent is a thing', and the 'aks (converse) is 'everything thing is existent'—these must both hold for the definition to be valid in both legal (shar'i) and rational contexts.

Key Terms
الاطراد
الانعكاس
جامع مانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The essential condition for a valid definition is that it must be perfectly coextensive with the defined term, neither broader, narrower, nor different.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Condition of Mutarid and Mun'akis in Definitions
Author explains the condition of coextensiveness and conversion for valid definitions with examples and critiques.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to test whether any definition in usul al-fiqh or kalam satisfies the conditions of coextensiveness (mutarid) and conversion (mun'akis), using the criteria explained by the usulis.

Core Idea
A valid definition must be exactly equal to the defined term—no broader, no narrower.

When jurists define a term like 'duty' or 'knowledge', they must ensure that the definition applies to every instance of the term and only to those instances. This is the condition of mutarid and mun'akis, explained by al-Shirazi and al-Juwayni, and applied by al-Tufi and al-Sam'ani to critique flawed definitions. In this lesson, you will learn to identify whether a definition meets this essential requirement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defined term and the definition
Locate the term being defined (e.g., duty/takleef, permissibility/ibaaha, knowledge/ilm) and the definition offered for it.
The definition is usually expressed as a phrase that is claimed to be equivalent.
2
Check for equality in extension
Ask: Does the definition include every instance of the defined term? (mutarid) Does it exclude everything that is not an instance? (mun'akis). If the definition is broader (includes non-instances) or narrower (excludes some instances), it fails.
Broader definitions violate mutarid; narrower definitions violate mun'akis.
3
Apply the test of coextensiveness (al-ittiraad)
For mutarid: verify that whenever the defined term exists, the definition also exists. For example, if duty is defined as 'command or prohibition', check if every duty is a command or prohibition (yes, but also check if every command/prohibition is a duty? That's for conversion). Mutarid requires: if X is duty, then X is command/prohibition.
This is one direction.
4
Apply the test of conversion (al-in'ikaas)
For mun'akis: verify that whenever the definition exists, the defined term also exists. If duty is defined as 'command/prohibition', check if every command/prohibition is a duty. Al-Tufi shows that commands like 'Eat and drink' are not duties, so conversion fails.
Both directions must hold for the definition to be valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد نص الأصوليون على ضرورة توفر الحد أو التعريف على جملة من الشروط الأساسية، وهي: أ - الاطراد والانعكاس: ومعناه؛ أن يكون مساويًا تمامًا للمفرد الذي نشرحه فلا يصح أن يكون أعم منه ، ولا أن يكون أخص منه، ولا أن يكون مباينا له، لأنه إذا كان أعم فإننا نجعل المخاطب يدخل في تصوره جزئيات هي خارجة عن المفرد الذي نشرحه له، وإذا كان أخص منه فإننا نجعل المخاطب يخرج في تصوره جزئيات هي داخلة في المفرد الذي نشرحه له، وإذا كان مباينا له فإننا نشرح الشيء بمغاير له تماما وبذلك نعطيه صورة غير صحيحة، ونوقعه في تصور خاطئ مخالف للواقع والحقيقة. من كل هذا يتبين لنا أنه يشترط في التعريف أن يكون مساويا تماما للمفرد الذي نشرحه به، ويعبرون عن هذا الشرط بقولهم : يشترط في التعريف أن يكون جامعا مانعا وبقولهم: يشترط في التعريف أن يكون مطردا منعكسا قال الشيرازي : من شرط الحد أن يطرد وينعكس، فيوجد المحدود بوجوده، ويعدم بعدمه . ومتى لم يكن كذلك لم يكن حدًا، وينطبق على المحدود انطباق الكف على الكف لا يفضل أحدهما على الآخر. وقال الجويني : من حكم الحد؛ أن يكون مشروطا بالعكس لا محالة شرع كان أو عقليّا، وهو أن تقول في حد الشيء : إنه الموجود، فطرده أن تقول : كل شيء موجود وعكسه أن تقول : وكل موجود شيء. لذلك فإن التعريف يعتبر ناقصًا إذا اختل فيه هذان الشرطان . ذلك ما علق به الطوفي على تعريف مصطلح «الإباحة»، قال: اختلف الأصوليون في الإباحة، هل هي تكليف أم لا؟. فإن قلنا ليست تكليفا صح تعريف التكليف بما ذكر، فيكون كل تكليف خطابا بأمر أو نهي وكل خطاب بأمر أو نهي تكليفا. وإن قلنا : الإباحة تكليف، انتقض التعريف المذكور بها من جهة الطرد، وهو وجود الحد بدون المحدود، لأنه ليس كلما وجد الخطاب بأمر أو الخطاب بأمر أو نهي وجد التكليف، لأن الإباحة مثل قوله : وَكُلُوا وَاشْرَبُوا فَإِذَا طَعِمْتُمْ فَانتَشِرُوا وَإِذَا حَلَلْتُمْ فَأَصْطَادُوا ونحوه من المباحات، خطاب بأمر وليس تكليفا . وكذلك من جهة العكس، وهو انتفاء المحدود عند انتفاء الحد، لأنه ليس كلما انتفى الخطاب بأمر أو نهي قد ينتفي، ويكون التكليف موجودا في المباحات، إذ حقيقة الإباحة : التخيير بين الفعل وتركه نحو إن شئت فافعل، وإن شئت لا تفعل ؛ وحقيقة التخيير غير حقيقة الأمر والنهي . فإذا قلنا : الإباحة تكليف، فقد صح وجود التكليف مع انتفاء الأمر والنهي. ومن ذلك أيضا ما انتقد به السمعاني تعريف المعتزلة للعلم : والذي قاله بعضهم - ويعني بهم المعتزلة - : إنه (أي العلم)؛ اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به باطل؛ لأن الله تعالى عالم بعلم على ما نطق الكتاب والسنة، ولا يطلق عليه الاعتقاد بحال، بل هو من صفات المخلوقين. وإذا لم يكن الحد جامعا، لم يكن صحيحا.
And the legal theorists insisted on the necessity that the definition or description fulfill a number of basic conditions, namely: (a) al-ittiraad (coextensiveness) and al-in'ikaas (conversion). Its meaning: that it be completely equal to the term we are explaining; it is not correct for it to be more general than it, nor more specific than it, nor disjoint from it. Because if it is more general, we cause the interlocutor to include in his conception particulars that are outside the term we are explaining to him. If it is more specific, we cause the interlocutor to exclude in his conception particulars that are inside the term we are explaining to him. If it is disjoint, we explain the thing by something completely different from it, and thereby give him an incorrect picture and cause him to have an erroneous conception contrary to reality and truth. From all this it becomes clear to us that it is required in the definition that it be completely equal to the term we are explaining. They express this condition by saying: 'It is required in the definition that it be comprehensive (jaami') and exclusive (maani')' and by saying: 'It is required in the definition that it be coextensive (mutarid) and convertible (mun'akis).' Al-Shiraazi said: 'One condition of the definition (hadd) is that it be coextensive and convertible: the defined exists with its existence and ceases with its non-existence. When it is not like that, it is not a definition; and it applies to the defined like the palm applied to the palm, neither one exceeding the other.' Al-Juwayni said: 'Among the properties of the definition is that it must necessarily be conditional upon conversion, whether shar'i or rational. That you say in the definition of a thing: it is existent; its coextensiveness is that you say: 'Everything existent is a thing', and its conversion is that you say: 'Everything thing is existent'.' Therefore, the definition is considered deficient if these two conditions are violated. That is what al-Tufi commented on the definition of the term 'ibaaha (permissibility)'. He said: 'The legal theorists differed about ibaaha: is it a duty (takleef) or not? If we say it is not a duty, then the definition of duty as mentioned is correct: every duty is a command or prohibition, and every command or prohibition is a duty. But if we say ibaaha is a duty, the mentioned definition is refuted from the aspect of coextensiveness, which is the existence of the definition without the defined, because not every time there is a command or prohibition is there a duty, because ibaaha like His saying 'Eat and drink' and 'When you have eaten, disperse' and 'When you have become lawful, hunt' and the like of permissible acts are commands but not duties. And likewise from the aspect of conversion, which is the non-existence of the defined when the definition is absent, because not every time the command or prohibition is absent does duty also become absent, and duty exists in permissible acts, since the reality of ibaaha is the option between action and abandonment, like 'if you wish, do it, and if you wish, don't do it'; and the reality of option is different from the reality of command and prohibition. So if we say ibaaha is a duty, then duty exists while command and prohibition are absent.' And from that also is what al-Sam'ani criticized the Mu'tazila's definition of knowledge: 'And what some of them said - meaning the Mu'tazila - that knowledge is belief of a thing as it is, is false; because God is knowing with knowledge as the Book and Sunna state, and belief is never applied to Him, rather it is among the attributes of creatures. And if the definition is not comprehensive (jaami'), it is not correct.'
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage defines the condition of mutarid and mun'akis for valid definitions. It opens by stating that scholars of usul insist on basic conditions for definition, the first being coextensiveness and conversion. The author explains that the definition must be exactly equal to the defined term—neither more general, nor more specific, nor disjoint. He gives the reasoning: a broader definition includes extraneous particulars; a narrower definition excludes relevant particulars; a disjoint definition misleads entirely. The condition is expressed as 'jaami' maani'' (comprehensive and exclusive) and 'mutarid mun'akis'. Two classic definitions from Shirazi and Juwayni are quoted to illustrate. Then the author gives al-Tufi's critique of a definition of ibaaha that fails mutarid and mun'akis. Tufi shows that if ibaaha is considered a duty, then the definition of duty as command/prohibition fails mutarid because there are commands (like 'eat') that are not duties, and fails mun'akis because duty can exist without command/prohibition (since ibaaha is an option). Finally, al-Sam'ani's critique of the Mu'tazila's definition of knowledge as belief is presented: it is not jaami' because it excludes God's knowledge, since belief is not attributed to God.

Key Terms
الاطراد والانعكاس
الحد
التعريف
جامع مانع

A) Every existent is a thing, and every thing is existent.
B) Every human is an animal, and every animal is human.
C) Every intoxicant is forbidden, and every forbidden is intoxicant.
D) Every created is temporal, and every temporal is created.

Key Takeaway
A definition that is not coextensive (mutarid mun'akis) is considered deficient and invalid in Islamic legal theory.
